        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer549322024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1452900467149044558">So I assume you have read the book description and know approximately what the book is about. This description ends with the words: "<u>The Painted Veil</u> is a beautifully written affirmation of the human capacity to grow, to change, and to forgive." Well, I would say that this can certainly be debated! This is an ideal book for a book club; there is much that can be discussed. Views will differ. <br/><br/>Some people find this book a huge disappointment. Others like it very much. So how do you go about decidi</span>
  <span id="freeText1452900467149044558" style="display:none">So I assume you have read the book description and know approximately what the book is about. This description ends with the words: "<u>The Painted Veil</u> is a beautifully written affirmation of the human capacity to grow, to change, and to forgive." Well, I would say that this can certainly be debated! This is an ideal book for a book club; there is much that can be discussed. Views will differ. <br /><br />Some people find this book a huge disappointment. Others like it very much. So how do you go about deciding whether to read it or not? For me the answer is simple. If you need to like the characters in a book, then I doubt this book will suit you. If you need characters to grow wiser, mature and improve themselves then your opinion will be up for grabs. If you want a book that draws realistic characters that do what a person with that personality would do, then yeah, I think you’ll like this. This is why I liked it. This is my favorite by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4176632.W__Somerset_Maugham" title="W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">W. Somerset Maugham</a>.<br /><br />The author states in the short introduction that this is the only one of his books where he started with an idea for a plot and thereafter invented characters to fit the events. Usually he began in the opposite end, with a character or characters around which he constructed a plot. This worried me since character portrayal is more important to me than plot! Don't worry as I did. Every character is exceedingly well drawn. Just as with real, live people, no two are alike. Each one has a very specific and clear personality. Each one's actions and words and thoughts correspond perfectly with their different personalities. This is why I so liked the book. I repeatedly stopped and considered how would such a person behave? Always their actions rang true. OK, one may not like the actions, but what is delivered is how people really are. There are two nuns. They help balance the shallowness found in some, the anger and inability to forgive found in another. Most significantly, the characters are utterly real. Real because you understand them and because each is a mix of good and bad qualities. <br /><br />Description of places occasionally took my breath away. Description of clothes, facial appearances and manners are astute. Superb dialogs. What I am saying is that the writing, the language used, is exceptionally good! Sometimes lyrical, most often precise. <br /><br />I listened to the audiobook narrated by Joanna Ward, alias Kate Reading. Don’t read the paper book. Don’t listen to an audiobook narrated by another; Reading’s narration is so magnificently well done. Perfect - perfect speed, excellent clarity and exceptionally well interpreted intonations that reflect the respective characters’ personalities. <br /><br />I never imagined I would enjoy this so very, very much. Honestly, there is nothing I can think of to complain about. Four stars correspond perfectly with my reaction to the book. <br /><br />******************************<br /><br />Other books I have read by Maugham with their respective ratings:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/397539.Christmas_Holiday" title="Christmas Holiday by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Christmas Holiday</a> 3 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a> 2 stars<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44796.The_Moon_and_Sixpence" title="The Moon and Sixpence by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Moon and Sixpence</a> 2 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="1452900467149044558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_11434080" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11434080" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/704819-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11434080?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/704819-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">friends, but I recommend the movie MORE!</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11434080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5772527928684669317">I agree whole-heartedly with other reviewers -- the movie was better! I saw the movie first and loved it. It is a brilliant and beautiful love story -- and who can resist Edward Norton's stoic, yet smoldering interpretation of Walter. <br/><br/>You can appreciate the movie better once you read the book and get to know the characters as they were originally intended. The movie does a good job of interpreting those characters honestly and uses dialogue verbatim from the book. But, in the movie, Walter and K</span>
  <span id="freeText5772527928684669317" style="display:none">I agree whole-heartedly with other reviewers -- the movie was better! I saw the movie first and loved it. It is a brilliant and beautiful love story -- and who can resist Edward Norton's stoic, yet smoldering interpretation of Walter. <br /><br />You can appreciate the movie better once you read the book and get to know the characters as they were originally intended. The movie does a good job of interpreting those characters honestly and uses dialogue verbatim from the book. But, in the movie, Walter and Kitty are much more likeable -- probably because they find (SPOILER ALERT)redemption in their love for one another, and Kitty is able to resist Charlie's later advances.<br /><br />While the book was well-written, I was expecting a similar ending to the movie and was all the more disappointed with Kitty in particular. I would have liked to experience more resolution between Kitty and Walter, but she was trying to be honest -- and if she truly did not love him, it would not have been fair to expect her to say she did. It was still disappointing. Equally, if not more, disappointing still was the way Kitty was able to dismiss her experiences with Walter and the epidemic as part of another world -- how, far too quickly, she embraced the luxuries and comforts of life back in Hong Kong. But, perhaps, this was more realistic.<br />Even still, it is the moment I became less forgiving of her character and the direction the book was taking. I would have liked her to have still more sympathy and love for her family, became more transparent with Waddington and, finally, resist Charlie -- even one last time. She didn't change as much as I had hoped. <br /><br />The book did have one very redemptive moment for me at the end -- Kitty told her father that (SPOILER ALERT) she would raise her daughter to be self-sufficient. I did not expect the book to have such a clear message. It made a lot of sense, but still did not entirely redeem Kitty for me. She experienced moments of truth, which were so revealing, but ultimately more frustrating than they were helpful. I only hope she was able to raise her daughter to be more compassionate and less self-absorbed -- but, for a character like Kitty, it seems unlikely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5772527928684669317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11562315792862691482" style="display:none"><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1456235442ra/18207213.gif" width="500" height="156" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'll start by saying that I had seen <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Painted_Veil_%282006_film%29" rel="nofollow noopener">the movie</a> before finding out that it was based on a book. <div id="review_80671161" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80671161" />
    <a title="Tatiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tatiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tatiana" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=nostalgia">nostalgia</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80671161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15606527829684197841">There is something infinitely fascinating to me about stories like this - a clever, passionate man falling in love with a pretty but fickle woman who, he knows, will never understand and appreciate his worth, and yet, he can't resist. This is why I have always obsessed over <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_with_the_Wind' title='Gone with the Wind by Margaret Mitchell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gone With the Wind</a> and enjoyed reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99664.The_Painted_Veil' title='The Painted Veil by W. Somerset Maugham' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Painted Veil</a> once again.<br/><br/>I still can't decide if I like the novel or the latest movie adaptation more.<br/><br/>The movie gives food to my romantic <i>what-could-have-been</i> fantasies. <br/><br/><br/>But I can't n</span>
  <span id="freeText15606527829684197841" style="display:none">There is something infinitely fascinating to me about stories like this - a clever, passionate man falling in love with a pretty but fickle woman who, he knows, will never understand and appreciate his worth, and yet, he can't resist. This is why I have always obsessed over <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_with_the_Wind" title="Gone with the Wind by Margaret Mitchell" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone With the Wind</a> and enjoyed reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99664.The_Painted_Veil" title="The Painted Veil by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Veil</a> once again.<br /><br />I still can't decide if I like the novel or the latest movie adaptation more.<br /><br />The movie gives food to my romantic <i>what-could-have-been</i> fantasies. <br /><br /><br />But I can't not love the novel and Kitty for this:<br /><br /><i>When I look back upon the girl I was I hate myself. But I never had a chance. I'm going to bring up my daughter so that she's free and can stand on her own feet. I'm not going to bring a child into the world, and love her, and bring her up, just so that some man may want to sleep with her so much that he's willing to provide her with board and lodging for the rest of her life.</i><br /><br />Makes those "clever" men seem utterly foolish and selfish after all...</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_318213928" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/318213928" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/318213928?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140008722" href="/book/show/824855.The_Painted_Veil">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Catie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer318213928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11603610201746306662">There’s something so affecting about tragedy created by social mores – tragedy that only exists because it’s born in a certain place and a certain time. While reading this book I just kept thinking, what if? What if women were allowed to seek their own status and power and weren’t completely dependent on that of their husbands? What if girls weren’t isolated but allowed to travel and gain experience or an education? What if marriage were optional? What if divorce were a simple thing? But I guess</span>
  <span id="freeText11603610201746306662" style="display:none">There’s something so affecting about tragedy created by social mores – tragedy that only exists because it’s born in a certain place and a certain time. While reading this book I just kept thinking, what if? What if women were allowed to seek their own status and power and weren’t completely dependent on that of their husbands? What if girls weren’t isolated but allowed to travel and gain experience or an education? What if marriage were optional? What if divorce were a simple thing? But I guess even the possible solutions to these hypotheticals wouldn’t remove all the sadness from this book. What if we weren’t all born with a severely limited worldview? would be a better question, but of course that’s just a permanent part of the human condition.<br /><br />Kitty Fane, in many respects, has the worldview of a small child, and that is her tragedy. She was bred to make an advantageous marriage – to finally realize all of her mother’s long repressed ambitions. She was raised in a narrow, cosseted, ridiculously skewed environment and so became narrow, cosseted, and ridiculously skewed. Kitty doesn’t want to be married, but after several seasons out and no husband, the pressure to choose someone increases. Desperate to appease her mother and beat her younger sister to the altar, she accepts the hand of quiet, intellectual bacteriologist Walter who seems to have genuine feeling for her, even though he barely knows her. <br /><br />Kitty is foolish, selfish, immature, and closed-minded. She doesn’t love Walter – in fact – he bores and irritates her. Once in Walter’s assigned post of Hong Kong, she begins an affair with the charming and middle aged (not to mention, married) Charles Townsend. And that’s where this book opens: a bedroom, a locked door, two lovers, and a humiliated husband.<br /><br />I admit that I loved Kitty from the beginning, even though her choices were maddening. She’s weak-willed and horribly naïve, but she does have the good sense to realize when she’s been wrong. And she has the courage to try to change herself, even though she doesn’t always succeed. Maugham doesn’t reform her completely and I think she’s all the more sympathetic for that. She fights against her own barriers: against her weakness and selfishness and outright disgust of the Chinese, but she never becomes a perfect, enlightened adult.<br /><br />I think that one of the hallmarks of adulthood is that moment when you feel like you’ve become stable: when you finally feel like you’ve learned a few things. You finally feel mature. And then something comes along and casually knocks you off your feet and you realize that you still don’t know a damn thing. Kitty experiences that completely, and I think it’s a huge testament to how much maturity she gains that she picks herself up, brushes herself off, and keeps on going. Her mistakes in this book actually made me love her even more, because she doesn’t let them define her.<br /><br />Nor is Walter portrayed as the simple, wounded hero. This story really is about Kitty and Kitty’s journey toward maturity but Walter is by no means a side character. It frankly amazed me just how vivid and three-dimensional he felt, given that he’s barely in the novel at all! Maugham was able to paint Walter so completely in brief, concentrated scenes (“<i>It was the dog that died.</i>” Talk about a punch in the face!). But of course, Walter is a quiet person: he speaks in actions or silences and rarely ever with words. Although, his one major speech in the book, where he basically tells Kitty exactly where she can go, is very satisfying:<br /><br /><i>"I knew you were silly and frivolous and empty-headed. But I loved you. I knew that your aims and ideals were vulgar and commonplace. But I loved you. I knew that you were second-rate. But I loved you. It's comic when I think how hard I tried to be amused by the things that amused you and how anxious I was to hide from you that I wasn't ignorant and vulgar and scandal-mongering and stupid. I knew how frightened you were of intelligence and I did everything I could to make you think me as big a fool as the rest of the men you knew. I knew that you'd only married me for convenience. I loved you so much, I didn't care."</i><br /><br />He’s shy and self-contained with little to no charm. He’s sensitive and passionate, but when wronged that passion becomes an ugly thing. He becomes cold and he can’t forgive. He’s far from the meek fool that Charles Townsend assumes he is and he’s also not the sweet loving husband that Kitty thought she married. <br /><br />However, Walter’s love for Kitty is just as naïve as her love for Charles Townsend, and in the end it is she (not he – “the dog”) who has the resiliency and bravery to forgive and try to move forward. When set against the wildness of rural China and the indiscriminate brutality of cholera, their doomed relationship becomes even more of a pointless tragedy. <br /><br /><a href="http://www.thereadventurer.com/1/post/2012/04/she-made-me-do-it-round-one.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Tatiana challenged me to read this book</a> and I’m so glad she did!<br /><br />Also seen on <a href="http://www.thereadventurer.com/1/post/2012/05/adult-audiobook-review-the-painted-veil-by-w-somerset-maugham.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Readventurer</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11603610201746306662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review318213928">

<div id="review_3171162927" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3171162927" />
    <a title="PorshaJo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo"><img alt="PorshaJo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451425289p2/5464046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3171162927?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PorshaJo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PorshaJo" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo">PorshaJo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads-2020">challengereads-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads">challengereads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3171162927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3916364662831656876">Rating 4.5<br/><br/>Why did I wait so long to read this one? I've been wanting to read this for so long, but so many books. I had a copy of the movie version, but refused to watch it until I read the book. And so the book and movie sat for years. Recently I was sick in bed and wanted to watch a movie and finally gave in to to this one. At the end of the movie, I immediately went to my library online and grabbed the audio and started it the next day. Guess you always need some kind of motivation. <br/><br/>Kitty Fan</span>
  <span id="freeText3916364662831656876" style="display:none">Rating 4.5<br /><br />Why did I wait so long to read this one? I've been wanting to read this for so long, but so many books. I had a copy of the movie version, but refused to watch it until I read the book. And so the book and movie sat for years. Recently I was sick in bed and wanted to watch a movie and finally gave in to to this one. At the end of the movie, I immediately went to my library online and grabbed the audio and started it the next day. Guess you always need some kind of motivation. <br /><br />Kitty Fane is a spoiled brat who thinks nothing more than herself. One day she meets Walter Fane who is immediately taken with her and asks for her hand in marriage. Kitty agrees, not because she loves him, but for selfish reasons and saving face. They move from Britain to Hong Kong, where he's a bacteriologist. Kitty is more interested in parties and socializing, and it's here where she has an affair. The affair is with an absolute scoundrel who also thinks nothing more than himself. But Kitty is such a child and just wants to be loved by him. Walter discovers the affair and almost forces her to go with him to the heart of a cholera epidemic in China. Kitty has no love for her husband, but due to shame, and no where else to go, she follows Walter. Here is where Kitty really begins to grow up. Walter now loathes Kitty but he is starting to see her in another light. But he is so driven in helping the people affected with cholera and trying to save those who live there. I'll not say more to ruin the story.<br /><br />The book and movie were pretty close, until the end. It's a tough one but the endings were quite different and I might have to go with the movie version ending. I picked this one up via audio and I so enjoyed the narration. I felt transported while listening to this one. I was so immersed in the story (even though I knew it from the movie) that at times I often forgot what I was doing. I still keep thinking of the beautiful writing, the story, and the characters. I do see myself revisiting this one again some day. And now, I'm off to try more from Maugham. A highly suggested read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3916364662831656876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_358234081" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/358234081" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/358234081?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer358234081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18445450775599403543"><br/><br/>Maugham handles language beautifully, telling the nice, compact story of a vacuous socialite who doesn't take life serious enough and finds herself in a very serious situation. <br/><br/>His economy of word fails his purpose only once, but it is an important failure and mars <i>The Painted Veil</i> in a way that diminishes it enough to keep it from attaining the echelon of "masterpiece" status. Our heroine Kitty's transformation <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[her admission of her own worthlessness, willingness to admit it to an</span></span>
  <span id="freeText18445450775599403543" style="display:none"><br /><br />Maugham handles language beautifully, telling the nice, compact story of a vacuous socialite who doesn't take life serious enough and finds herself in a very serious situation. <br /><br />His economy of word fails his purpose only once, but it is an important failure and mars <i>The Painted Veil</i> in a way that diminishes it enough to keep it from attaining the echelon of "masterpiece" status. Our heroine Kitty's transformation <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[her admission of her own worthlessness, willingness to admit it to an "enemy," and attempts to change <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> happens way too fast to be believable. Waugham painted himself into a corner by forcing nature <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Kitty's pregnancy <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> into the story. It made him speed up the timeline to get it all in under the gun.<br /><br />Still, this is a well-told, highly enjoyable tale revolving around some good, bad and ambivalent people - most of which are all three of those at once - and as a water-tester for whether or not I wanted to throw myself into Maugham's much deeper work, <i>Of Human Bondage</i>, <i>The Painted Veil</i> was a success. A worthy good read indeed! </span>
  <a data-text-id="18445450775599403543" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2804579925" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2804579925" />
    <a title="Quo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7392004-quo"><img alt="Quo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393910036p2/7392004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2804579925?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quo" href="/user/show/7392004-quo">Quo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400034215" href="/book/show/395183.The_Painted_Veil">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone keen on the British Colonial experience or an exploration of Maughan's  skill as a novelist.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Quo by: </span>
      Local library book discussion selection
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=british-lit">british-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2804579925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16749530047257888143">Early on in Somerset Maugham's novel, <i> The Painted Veil</i>, one of the more interesting but peripheral characters, a District Commissioner named Waddington, stationed up-country from British colonial Hong Kong at Mei-Tan Fu, an outpost plagued by cholera intones: "I've asked myself if you're both here to commit suicide?" The couple in question are Walter Fane, M.D., a bacteriologist & his wife Kitty and they have recently come from Hong Kong to this infected site because Walter has chosen it as a k</span>
  <span id="freeText16749530047257888143" style="display:none">Early on in Somerset Maugham's novel, <i> The Painted Veil</i>, one of the more interesting but peripheral characters, a District Commissioner named Waddington, stationed up-country from British colonial Hong Kong at Mei-Tan Fu, an outpost plagued by cholera intones: "I've asked myself if you're both here to commit suicide?" The couple in question are Walter Fane, M.D., a bacteriologist &amp; his wife Kitty and they have recently come from Hong Kong to this infected site because Walter has chosen it as a kind of exile, having recently discovered that Kitty has been having an affair with a fellow named Charlie Townsend, the sort of man the British used to label somewhat redundantly a "cad &amp; a bounder". <br><br>So continues a story that begins with the pair seeming to have little or nothing in common back in London &amp; having a conversation that demonstrates that they are almost uniquely incapable of communicating with each other, though Walter has declared himself to be deeply in love with Kitty, who is perplexed by his shy, halting declaration. However, after calling him an "odd creature", she then indicates that "his impassivity was vaguely impressive" &amp; though she has entertained other would-be suitors, no man has ever seemed so in love with her. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1582682081i/29020272._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The early dialogue between them appears laughable, a display of the kind of alienation that normally takes years for a couple to achieve but Walter is soon to leave for Hong Kong and Kitty wishes to escape her family home where a younger sister has already long since married &amp; another is about to marry. Beyond that, she had nothing else on her calendar for the ensuing months, or so it seems. It might be seen as a caricature of a "very British marriage" in days past, particularly when women had few professional outlets &amp; the clock was ticking as it were.<br><br>After their impromptu marriage &amp; Walter's discovery of Kitty's intimate relationship with another man, the couple now face both isolation &amp; the threat of cholera + other disease at the outpost of Mei-Tan-Fu when Charlie Townsend refuses to leave his own wife and bond with Kitty in Hong Kong for fear of jeopardizing his career. While Kitty is deeply in love with Charley, she has misjudged her options &amp; must either return to England in disgrace or follow her husband into harm's way. <br><br>Having fled Hong Kong with his wife in tow, Dr. Fane loses himself in his work with the sick people in the village, while Kitty has little refuge or support, excepting the alcoholic D.C. named Waddington. Still, Walter &amp; Kitty do at times seem to make an effort at coming to terms with a relationship that has never really been subscribed to. For...<blockquote>Why could he not realize that beside all the terror of death under whose shadow they lay &amp; beside the awe of the beauty which she had only caught a glimpse of, their own affairs were trivial? What did it really matter that a silly woman had committed adultery &amp; why should her husband, face to face with the sublime, give it a thought? It was strange that Walter with all of his cleverness should have so little sense of proportion. <br><br>Because he had dressed a doll in gorgeous robes &amp; set her in a sanctuary to worship her &amp; then discovered that the doll was filled with sawdust, he could neither forgive himself nor her. His soul was lacerated. It was all make-believe that he had lived on, &amp; when the truth shattered it, he thought reality itself was shattered. He could not forgive her because he could not forgive himself.</blockquote>Meanwhile, Kitty "had nothing else to do &amp; after looking for so many days at the walled city across the water, she was not unwilling to have at least a glimpse of its mysterious streets." She discovers a French convent tending to orphans and serving as a dispensary for villagers afflicted with cholera. The nuns are in awe of her husband's tireless work with the sick &amp; some of that admiration transfers to Kitty, attempting to see Walter as others view him, at the same time also gradually becoming transfixed by the nuns' steadfast sense of devotion to God while so very far from their native French soil. Mother Superior tells Kitty that "one does not find peace in work or in a convent but only in one's soul." <br><br>The sensitive portrait of the nuns &amp; that of Kitty, bereft of love, in transition &amp; searching for meaning is really quite vivid &amp; captivating. Kitty is stunned by the suffering she sees, noticing a man whose life is "being throttled out of him in the dark clutch of the pestilence", with the beauty of nature contrasted with the seeming indifference of life when "men were writhing in agony &amp; going to their death in fear." However, Kitty begins to plug into life at the convent, offering her assistance with the orphans, feeling a sense of purpose, perhaps for the first time in her life, with the nuns...<blockquote>wonderful &amp; so kind and yet--I hardly know how to explain it--there is a wall between them &amp; me. It is as though they possessed a secret which made all the difference in their lives &amp; which I was unworthy to share. It is not faith; it is something deeper &amp; more significant: they walk in a different world from ours &amp; we shall always be strangers to them. Each day when the convent door closes behind me, I feel that for them I have ceased to exist.</blockquote>And yet, the nuns do care for Kitty &amp; are in her debt, just as they feel indebted for husband Walter's unswerving work as well. Beyond that, the celibate nuns quickly notice that Kitty is pregnant, something she seemed unaware of herself. Somerset Maughan seems to aptly describe Kitty's feelings &amp; in really memorable passages details her continuing search for meaning, with a focus on the spirituality of the nuns and also the "immemorial, dark &amp; inscrutable beliefs of the East", some quality or experience that when found "would make all the difference." This is a tale of transformation &amp; one either buys into it, as I did, or not. Some may find the tale melodramatic or unconvincing but I was charmed by the strength of Maugham's language, with passages such as...<blockquote>New ideas flitted about her heart like yellow butterflies in the sunshine &amp; she hoped to be so much better in the future; freedom like a spirit of light had beckoned her on &amp; the world was like a spacious plain through which she could walk light of foot with head erect.</blockquote>Without wishing to give away the reason to potential readers of the novel, Kitty decides to book passage on a P&amp;O steamship bound for London. At novel's end, I was uplifted by Kitty's reunion with her father, wherein Kitty, following disembarkation just after the death of her imperious mother, attempts to do with her seemingly detached father that which she was unable to do with her husband Walter. <br><br>Will Kitty's plan work out?? Yes I say, especially if I am permitted to add a sequel of my own devising. In any case, I turned the last page of Somerset Maugham's <i>The Painted Veil</i> with a great sense of optimism for Kitty &amp; her pending child, as well as joy that I had chanced to read this early (1925) novel by Maugham, even if I found <i>The Razor's Edge</i> somewhat more engaging!<br><br>For what it is worth, Somerset Maugham was bisexual (he was married &amp; had a daughter though mostly slanted towards men), bi-professional (having been trained as a medical doctor but making his living as a novelist &amp; playwright), bi-national (having been born in France but within the British embassy there) &amp; bilingual. Actually, since he studied at the University of Heidelberg, he was likely trilingual. <br><br>Maugham was not however ambidextrous but one wonders if his dualities somehow allowed the author to visualize countering points of view &amp; to create compelling characters very unlike himself, Kitty Fane for example. Maugham was an agnostic but seemed able to craft an engaging portrayal of what inspired the French Catholic nuns to dedicate their lives to helping the poor &amp; needy in China. <br><br>I believe that Dr. Fane's last words: <i>The dog it was that died</i>, from a 1766 Oliver Goldsmith poem, "An Elegy on the Death of a Mad Dog" provide some terminal insight (so to speak) into the author's view of Kitty's husband. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1582682081i/29020273.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>*There are at least two film versions of <i>The Painted Veil</i>, including a 1934 movie with Greta Garbo as Kitty in an adaptation that takes great liberties with the Maugham novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16749530047257888143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2204543566?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucy Banks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucy Banks" href="/user/show/57486568-lucy-banks">Lucy Banks</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2204543566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13480859145532292868"><b>A casual read, that turned into a thoroughly enjoyable experience!</b><br/><br/>I only had this book because of my late Aunt, whose shelves were every bit as overflowing with books as my own. I'd obviously heard of the author, but really wasn't familiar with his style, so went in with no expectations whatsoever. <br/><br/>Oh my gosh, what a pleasant surprise to find out that it was utterly fantastic. <br/><br/>It's about Kitty, a young woman who has somehow found herself married to a man she's not even fond of, much less in love</span>
  <span id="freeText13480859145532292868" style="display:none"><b>A casual read, that turned into a thoroughly enjoyable experience!</b><br /><br />I only had this book because of my late Aunt, whose shelves were every bit as overflowing with books as my own. I'd obviously heard of the author, but really wasn't familiar with his style, so went in with no expectations whatsoever. <br /><br />Oh my gosh, what a pleasant surprise to find out that it was utterly fantastic. <br /><br />It's about Kitty, a young woman who has somehow found herself married to a man she's not even fond of, much less in love with. The book starts with her affair - or rather, her fear of discovery, as she hears an ominous rattle on the door handle whilst still in bed with her lover. <br /><br />What follows next is unexpected - a passive aggressive response from her husband and a swift removal to a country riddled with cholera. Had her husband moved her there to kill her? And if so, will he succeed?<br /><br />Without ruining the story - the ending is unexpected and poignant. Kitty, though by no means a particularly sympathetic character (spoiled and a bit empty-headed about sums it up), has a glorious resilience, and manages to find her own way in the world, sans any men. Although of its time, there's a definite note of female strength here, which naturally resonated with me strongly. (I do love a strong female lead!). <br /><br />Yes, there's regrettable racism in this book, which made me wince every time I encountered it - but I suppose we must remember that the author was a product of his time. For the most part, ugly racial stereotypes aside, this is a beautiful exploration of the intricacies of relationships, the inequality of love, and the joy of finding hidden reserves of strength in oneself, even in times of peril. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13480859145532292868" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_214010697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/214010697" />
    <a title="Mayra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1967563-mayra"><img alt="Mayra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605329000p2/1967563.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/214010697?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mayra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mayra" href="/user/show/1967563-mayra">Mayra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099286875" href="/book/show/816761.The_Painted_Veil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1967563-mayra?shelf=excellent">excellent</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer214010697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7642098031320848024">I was so ready to fall head over heels for this book. I’ve always liked the 2006 movie and I just had no doubt in my mind this Maugham book sitting there in my shelf, beckoning, was just a thousand times better. <b>I knew it in my bones this would be amazing.</b><br/><br/>Now that I’m finished I suppose I would be a gigantic liar if I said I’m not at least a little <b>disappointed</b>. And it’s definitely the movie’s fault. If I had never watched it, I’m sure I would have liked this book a million times better. But Nor</span>
  <span id="freeText7642098031320848024" style="display:none">I was so ready to fall head over heels for this book. I’ve always liked the 2006 movie and I just had no doubt in my mind this Maugham book sitting there in my shelf, beckoning, was just a thousand times better. <b>I knew it in my bones this would be amazing.</b><br /><br />Now that I’m finished I suppose I would be a gigantic liar if I said I’m not at least a little <b>disappointed</b>. And it’s definitely the movie’s fault. If I had never watched it, I’m sure I would have liked this book a million times better. But Norton and Watts planted all these tiny little romantic hopes in my brain, and when I started reading this novel, in the back of my mind, I was expecting this brilliant, clever, amazing love story.<br /><br />So many strangely disappointed hopes, as this book turns out to be a shrewd, emotional and terribly depressing account of a woman’s awakening. I’m all for that, which makes me really sad I went into this with such deluded expectations. Love is lost, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[but there is little love regained <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, which makes up for such a gut-wrenching read – especially when you were anticipating so much romance.<br /><br />I have to admit Maugham’s prose was not especially brilliant in my eyes. But the witty, fast-paced, immaculate <b>dialogue</b> in this book is what makes it so special to me. I’ve tried my hand at writing and I’ve seen how immensely difficult it is to write polished dialogue, so it’s truly impressive to read Walter and Kitty’s flawlessly crafted and effortless interactions.<br /><br />Of course, I had to cringe every time there was any portrayal of the Chinese, because it was truly, and bitterly <b>racist</b>. But what surprised me the most (pleasantly) was the <b>feminism</b>. Especially coming from a male writer in the ‘20s. I love it that the only extremely, openly sexist line in the novel was uttered by the one character deemed common and worthless.<br /><br />And I also love these:<br /><br /><i> “How silly men were! Their part in procreation was so unimportant; it was the woman who carried the child through long months of uneasiness and bore it with pain, and yet a man because of his momentary connection made such preposterous claims.”<br /><br />“When I look back upon the girl I was I hate myself. But I never had a chance. I’m going to bring up my daughter so that she’s free and can stand on her own feet. I’m not going to bring a child into the world, and love her, and bring her up, just so that some man may want to sleep with her so much that he’s willing to provide her with board and lodging for the rest of her life.” </i><br /><br />I only regret having dived into this novel with such clouded expectations, because even though it’s extremely real in its heartache, and it leaves you feeling hopelessly depressed, it is so mind-numbingly <b>worthwhile</b>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7642098031320848024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_15572439" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15572439" />
    <a title="Martine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/381149-martine"><img alt="Martine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1189863081p2/381149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15572439?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martine" href="/user/show/381149-martine">Martine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780749304300" href="/book/show/1823995.The_Painted_Veil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=early-twentieth-century">early-twentieth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=modern-fiction">modern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=film">film</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/381149-martine?shelf=psychological-drama">psychological-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15572439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17102578766105383201">What do you do when you discover that the wife you love despite the fact that she is shallow as hell and obviously despises you is having an affair? For Walter Fane, a bacteriologist working in early-twentieth-century colonial Hong Kong, the choice is easy. Either he will divorce his wife, which will disgrace her and leave her destitute (she was never taught to work or be independent, having always been expected to make a brilliant marriage), or, as a penance, she will have to accompany him on a</span>
  <span id="freeText17102578766105383201" style="display:none">What do you do when you discover that the wife you love despite the fact that she is shallow as hell and obviously despises you is having an affair? For Walter Fane, a bacteriologist working in early-twentieth-century colonial Hong Kong, the choice is easy. Either he will divorce his wife, which will disgrace her and leave her destitute (she was never taught to work or be independent, having always been expected to make a brilliant marriage), or, as a penance, she will have to accompany him on a trip to Southern China, where he has volunteered to work as a doctor in a cholera-ridden area from which few people return alive. Unable to face poverty and social disgrace, Kitty assents to her husband's proposal, convinced that he is hoping the trip will kill her. But is he really?<br /><br />Most authors would turn a melodramatic story like the above into a cliche-riddled soap opera in which husband and wife, out in the wilds, share some dramatic, eye-opening experiences and come to appreciate and love each other, after which the wife, having obtained her husband's forgiveness, dies a tearful death. For let's face it, who could resist a beautiful woman's death in such an exotic setting? To his credit, Maugham gives his readers a different ending, one in which Kitty is allowed to live and learn. Does she become a radically different person? Nope, she doesn't. Decades' worth of miseducation can't right a wrong like that. But she does gain a new insight into life in general and her life in particular, and tries to make amends for earlier wrongs. That's more than you expect from her at the beginning of the book, where she seems an entirely vapid and selfish being, another one of those horrid female characters Maugham drew so well.<br /><br />Whether or not you enjoy <i>The Painted Veil</i> largely depends on your tolerance for melodrama, unlikeable characters and racism, all of which abound in the book. My own tolerance for such things is substantial (at least in books -- not so much in real life), so I didn't mind the book too much. On the contrary, I found myself engrossed in the psychological details of the story -- the love, the loathing, the fear, the longing, the weakness, the growing awareness, the sense that forgiveness and redemption may be just around the corner. I also found myself (not for the first time) admiring Maugham's prodigious talent for characterisation. All the characters in the book, from the protagonists to the bit players, are fascinating and well drawn. I was intrigued by Walter, the quiet scientist who may or may not intend to let a horrible disease waste away his wife. I would have liked the story to be told more from his point of view, but then again, that would have made him less mysterious, which probably would have ruined the effect. Like many readers, I didn't overly care for Kitty, but I found it interesting to follow her journey and meet the men in her life. As I said, Maugham is a master of characterisation, and in <i>The Painted Veil</i> he presents some very interesting men. Charlie, Kitty's vain lover, is a superb creation, as is Waddington, an officer Kitty and Walter meet in China, who teaches Kitty that it's all right to defy society's expectations every now and then. The story itself may not be Maugham's best (I much prefer <i>Of Human Bondage</i>), but it certainly features memorable characters, as well as a social message which I'm sure was much needed at the time of writing. I can't wait to see the film adaptation, which apparently is even better than the book...</span>
  <a data-text-id="17102578766105383201" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1542670352" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1542670352" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1542670352?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=buddy-read">buddy-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=obscure-classics">obscure-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1542670352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8405660654572395050">I read this as a buddy read with my mother. This review is based on the reading notes I shared with her via e-mail. It was my third novel from Maugham; all have been excellent. Although he seems to have gone out of fashion, he was well respected in his time and at least a few of his books remain widely read. His titles are often taken from literary sources; here the title and epigraph are from a Shelley poem. Also, he explains in the preface that he got the idea for the plot from Dante’s <i>Purgato</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8405660654572395050" style="display:none">I read this as a buddy read with my mother. This review is based on the reading notes I shared with her via e-mail. It was my third novel from Maugham; all have been excellent. Although he seems to have gone out of fashion, he was well respected in his time and at least a few of his books remain widely read. His titles are often taken from literary sources; here the title and epigraph are from a Shelley poem. Also, he explains in the preface that he got the idea for the plot from Dante’s <i>Purgatorio</i> (and that he was forced to change the characters’ last name and make up a colony name to fill in for Hong Kong after threats of a libel suit). <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Walter’s last words are also a literary allusion: “The dog it was that died” comes from Oliver Goldsmith’s poem “An Elegy on the Death of a Mad Dog” (1766) – after a rabid dog bites a man, everyone thinks he will die. Instead, the dog dies. Walter is reflecting on the irony that he brought Kitty to this remote Chinese outpost so that she might die of cholera as punishment for her adultery, but he dies instead.<br /><br />It’s a sudden opening as Kitty and her lover, Charlie Townsend, are caught in the act. Stories of adulteresses were probably still fairly rare back in 1925. As in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2114433802" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>Madame Bovary</i></a> and <i>Anna Karenina</i>, the heroine must be punished for what she’s done. Maugham writes of “the sad perspicacity that she had learned in suffering,” a line that could also be applied to Bathsheba Everdene in Thomas Hardy’s <i>Far from the Madding Crowd</i>. From now on Kitty will live a life of duty (to her father), but she vows to raise her daughter to live independently of men. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Kitty’s mother, Mrs. Garstin, married for money and status and encouraged her daughter to do the same. Kitty knew within a few months that she’d made a mistake by marrying Walter, a microbiologist – “she was not quite at ease with him. He had no gaiety. … She had discovered very soon that he had an unhappy disability to lose himself. He was self-conscious.” <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[By contrast, Charlie “has the gift of making every one he meets feel that he is the one person in the world he wants to see.” We get an outsider’s view of Charlie through Waddington, and despite that described charisma Kitty’s view of her lover starts to change. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Maugham had a deep understanding of human psychology, male and female, and wrote magnificently about the search for purpose in a life ruled by fate. I loved the philosophical discussions between Kitty and Waddington about what matters and how to live well. Kitty says, “I’m looking for something and I don’t quite know what it is.” Waddington replies, “Some of us look for the Way in opium and some in God, some of us in whiskey and some in love.” Later he says, “I have an idea that the only thing which makes it possible to regard this world we live in without disgust is the beauty which now and then men create out of the chaos. The pictures they paint, the music they compose, the books they write, and the lives they lead. Of all these the richest in beauty is the beautiful life. That is the perfect work of art.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="8405660654572395050" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The Painted Veil a beautifully written story about a young wife, Kitty Fane, who is brought to Hong Kong by her doctor husband in the 1920's. She doesn't love her husband so she soon falls in love and begins an affair with her husbands superior. It's the story of how one-sided relationships can lead to heartbreak for everyone involved.   <span id="freeText7740208664598710196" style="display:none">I've always been amazed at the effort the British took to maintain their aristocratic traditions in their distant colonies. The Painted Veil a beautifully written story about a young wife, Kitty Fane, who is brought to Hong Kong by her doctor husband in the 1920's. She doesn't love her husband so she soon falls in love and begins an affair with her husbands superior. It's the story of how one-sided relationships can lead to heartbreak for everyone involved. This is a sad and tragic story but still beautiful and magical in the telling of it. 4.5 stars.</span>

<div id="review_73198239" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73198239" />
    <a title="Hannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2411388-hannah"><img alt="Hannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1272561978p2/2411388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73198239?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/2411388-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=2010-reads">2010-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer73198239&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73198239" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14673962098457574132">There's just so much to take in with this book that I hardly know where to begin. At first, the story of the feckless Kitty Fane reads so simplistically that I seriously wondered why Maugham was considered the brilliant writer that he was. It wasn't until Kitty and Walter began their tranformative journey to Mei-tan-fu that I began to sense why this is such a beloved book, in spite of the fact that Maugham creates and sets up two of the least lovable/admirable main characters since Kathy and Hea</span>
  <span id="freeText14673962098457574132" style="display:none">There's just so much to take in with this book that I hardly know where to begin. At first, the story of the feckless Kitty Fane reads so simplistically that I seriously wondered why Maugham was considered the brilliant writer that he was. It wasn't until Kitty and Walter began their tranformative journey to Mei-tan-fu that I began to sense why this is such a beloved book, in spite of the fact that Maugham creates and sets up two of the least lovable/admirable main characters since Kathy and Heathcliff. In spite of (or because of) that, the journey of Kitty and Walter Fane takes not only a physical toll to this damaged couple, but also a moral and spiritual one as well. As much as I loved this book, I would add that I question Maugham's ending, and personally feel that the movie adaptation presented a much better resolution then his more ambigious one. That was very likely his intent, but I confess that I preferred the more cut and dried morality tale set up by the screen writers. But I will also confess that Maugham's ending has kept me thinking of this book for 3 days straight after finishing it, so perhaps his way is, in actuality, the superior.<br /><br />In fact, Walter's dying words caused me to google the Oliver Goldsmith poem Walter references on his deathbed:<br /><br /><i><b>An Elegy on the Death of a Mad Dog</b><br /> <br /> Good people all, of every sort,<br /> Give ear unto my song;<br /> And if you find it wondrous short,<br /> It cannot hold you long.<br /><br /> In Islington there was a man,<br /> Of whom the world might say<br /> That still a godly race he ran,<br /> Whene'er he went to pray.<br /><br /> A kind and gentle heart he had,<br /> To comfort friends and foes;<br /> The naked every day he clad,<br /> When he put on his clothes.<br /><br /> And in that town a dog was found,<br /> As many dogs there be,<br /> Both mongrel, puppy, whelp and hound,<br /> And curs of low degree.<br /><br /> This dog and man at first were friends;<br /> But when a pique began,<br /> The dog, to gain some private ends,<br /> Went mad and bit the man.<br /><br /> Around from all the neighbouring streets<br /> The wondering neighbours ran,<br /> And swore the dog had lost his wits,<br /> To bite so good a man.<br /><br /> The wound it seemed both sore and sad<br /> To every Christian eye;<br /> And while they swore the dog was mad,<br /> They swore the man would die.<br /><br /> But soon a wonder came to light,<br /> That showed the rogues they lied:<br /> The man recovered of the bite,<br /> <b>The dog it was that died.</b></i><br /><br />hmm...much like Walter's last words, <i>The Painted Veil</i> might mystify, intrigue, and haunt the reader long after the last page is completed. It did for me.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14673962098457574132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2767415151" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2767415151" />
    <a title="Joshie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie"><img alt="Joshie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588954776p2/11973316.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2767415151?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshie" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie">Joshie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400034215" href="/book/show/395183.The_Painted_Veil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=literature-british-and-others">literature-british-and-others</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2767415151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6070047250241375742">Opens shockingly with a discovery of a nasty secret, a sin is willingly and recklessly consummated for adultery's sweet and irresistible forbidden fruit. Also a portrait of a dysfunctional marriage, between Kitty and Walter Fane, which rationalises its foundation, a deluded notion, that social status and money are most important. These melt morality into a swamp of revenge and hate; marrying other than love is often disastrous and dangerous. Unfortunately and frustratingly, society views the unf</span>
  <span id="freeText6070047250241375742" style="display:none">Opens shockingly with a discovery of a nasty secret, a sin is willingly and recklessly consummated for adultery's sweet and irresistible forbidden fruit. Also a portrait of a dysfunctional marriage, between Kitty and Walter Fane, which rationalises its foundation, a deluded notion, that social status and money are most important. These melt morality into a swamp of revenge and hate; marrying other than love is often disastrous and dangerous. Unfortunately and frustratingly, society views the unfaithful woman worse than the unfaithful man.<br /><br /><i>The Painted Veil</i> is an undeniable page-turner, its characters unlikable as the scorching heat of the sun at noon, but its simple prose, too simple, distracts with Maugham's awkward structures from time to time (on p224: ** "And then he found her lips and the pressure of his upon them shot through her body like the flame of God. It was an ecstasy and he was burnt to a cinder and she glowed as though she were transfigured." They're clearly doing the hanky-panky here and it's pretty blasphemous I can't help but laugh then on p244: ** "He kissed her, on the lips, as a lover might, and his cheeks were wet with her tears." It's a scene between Kitty and her father. That description wasn't necessary, was it. But Kitty's hunger for genuine love and attention, some psychoanalysis perhaps, can be seen from this.)<br /><br />Despite these strange wordings, the novel's strength lies in its vivid portrayal of a town struck by cholera whilst Kitty familiarises, punishes, and frightens herself with its culture. As a foreigner in a place colonised by her own native country, her initial prejudice on the Chinese (yellow skin, slanted eyes are described horrifically) wanes as each day she learns to live a life alien to her. Through the missionary nuns and the children in the orphanage residing in the same town, she learns the most important type of love: charity. Even the nuns themselves are not perfect and are also plagued by similar prejudices. They whisper and look down on an interracial relationship in the book. As there are hints of racism, there is no religious discrimination. <br /><br />By the end, this closes in a very hopeful manner. A hope for change and most importantly, the epiphany, which I think is very ahead for its time, that women don't have to rely on any man to live, be happy, and have what she needs. Some of the twists and turns of the novel are, amidst being predictable, engaging you feel very much involved with the characters' lives. As subtle as empowerment is in <i>The Painted Veil</i>, there is no denying it is an important character study of the unfaithful woman shamed and stripped.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6070047250241375742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('REfWuLhKcKqif0JjDXpNmv6BJYac9GhyP3Ps+ABEKxzmPHhFs3Uk0rm3541uFKuvfMjER3ndfrpTZ83lT5VAuw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ku3401urhbcCXUEfrECelg4gQARUxDGKaTnmpaSxFqIwllYuUJTRzxmV5PHPLnijjGmhxbHtJ0IFLce462B9BQ==')})
  }

//<![CDATA[

Le giovani coppie che vediamo amoreggiare in una notte primaverile (la Grande Guerra è finita da pochi mesi, e loro sono i fortunati, quelli che alla carneficina delle trincee sono riusciti a sopravvivere) hanno, apparentemente, un solo desiderio: godere, in una immediatezza senza domani, ignorando «il lato sordido» della vita, soffocando «la paura dell\'ombra». Eppure, quasi sulla soglia del romanzo, uno dei protagonisti si pone una domanda - «Come avviene, nel matrimonio, il passaggio dall\'amore all\'amicizia? Quando si smette di tormentarsi a vicenda e si comincia finalmente a volersi bene?» - che ne costituirà il filo conduttore. Con mano ferma, e con uno sguardo ironicamente compassionevole, Irène Némirovsky accompagna i suoi giovani personaggi, attraverso le intermittenze e le devastazioni della passione, fino alla quieta, un po\' ottusa sicurezza dell\'amore co niugale. The dogs are the comfortable, assimilated rich Jews up on the hill, while the wolves, their distant cousins, struggle below in the ghetto. Ada grows up motherless, looked after first by her father, then by an indomitable, social-climbing aunt, and eventually moves to Paris with her aunt\'s family, all of them looking for a brighter future. Ada makes a living in Paris as an artist, painting scenes from the world she has left behind. Her cousin Ben, intense and ferociously intelligent, has loved her for years; they share memories - together they survived the terrible pogroms of their childhood - and he presses her to marry him. But Harry Sinner is also in Paris, moving in exclusive circles, and infatuated with the daughter of a wealthy gentile banker. One day he buys two paintings which remind him of his past... and the course of Ada\'s life changes once more.\n\nBut as recession and revolutions shake previously rich regimes, even a solid international bank can find itself over-extended and vulnerable to greed. Ben, now working for the Sinner family bank in Paris, is well-placed to take the kind of risks he could only dream of in the past. And as summer draws to a close, Ada\'s world is disintegrating and she is faced with a fateful decision.\n\nThe Dogs and the Wolves, painted on a broad, vibrant canvas, with Némirovsky\'s acute eye
